[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Carter_Brezhnev_sign_SALT_II.jpg/220px-Carter_Brezhnev_sign_SALT_II.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carter_Brezhnev_sign_SALT_II.jpg)

Soviet general secretary [Leonid Brezhnev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonid_Brezhnev "Leonid Brezhnev") and US President [Jimmy Carter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jimmy_Carter "Jimmy Carter") sign the [SALT II arms limitation treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Treaty_II "Strategic Arms Limitation Treaty II") in Vienna on 18 June 1979.

Although indirect conflict between Cold War powers continued through the late 1960s and early 1970s, tensions were beginning to ease.<sup id="cite_ref-371"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-371">[353]</a></sup>

Following the ousting of Khrushchev, another period of [collective leadership](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collective_leadership "Collective leadership") ensued, consisting of Leonid Brezhnev as general secretary, [Alexei Kosygin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexei_Kosygin "Alexei Kosygin") as Premier and [Nikolai Podgorny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolai_Podgorny "Nikolai Podgorny") as Chairman of the Presidium, lasting until Brezhnev established himself in the early 1970s as the preeminent Soviet leader.

Following his visit to China, Nixon met with Soviet leaders, including Brezhnev in Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBBC1972_372-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC1972-372">[354]</a></sup> These [Strategic Arms Limitation Talks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks "Strategic Arms Limitation Talks") resulted in two landmark arms control treaties: [SALT I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks#SALT_I_Treaty "Strategic Arms Limitation Talks"), the first comprehensive limitation pact signed by the two superpowers, and the [Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-Ballistic_Missile_Treaty "Anti-Ballistic Missile Treaty"), which banned the development of systems designed to intercept incoming missiles. These aimed to limit the development of costly anti-ballistic missiles and nuclear missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarabell1999916_126-11"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarabell1999916-126">[120]</a></sup>

Nixon and Brezhnev proclaimed a new era of "peaceful coexistence" and established the groundbreaking new policy of [détente](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D%C3%A9tente "Détente") (or cooperation) between the two superpowers. Meanwhile, Brezhnev attempted to revive the Soviet economy, which was declining in part because of heavy military expenditures. The Soviet Union's [military budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_budget "Military budget") in the 1970s was gigantic, forming 40–60% of the entire federal budget and accounting to 15% of the USSR's GDP (13% in the 1980s).<sup id="cite_ref-373"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-373">[355]</a></sup> Between 1972 and 1974, the two sides also agreed to strengthen their economic ties,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber1993194–197_117-11"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber1993194%E2%80%93197-117">[111]</a></sup> including agreements for increased trade. As a result of their meetings, _détente_ would replace the hostility of the Cold War and the two countries would live mutually.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELitwak1986_374-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELitwak1986-374">[356]</a></sup> These developments coincided with [Bonn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bonn "Bonn")'s "[Ostpolitik](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ostpolitik "Ostpolitik")" policy formulated by the West German Chancellor [Willy Brandt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willy_Brandt "Willy Brandt"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005154_318-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005154-318">[301]</a></sup> an effort to normalize relations between West Germany and Eastern Europe. Other agreements were concluded to stabilize the situation in Europe, culminating in the [Helsinki Accords](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helsinki_Accords "Helsinki Accords") signed at the [Conference on Security and Co-operation in Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conference_on_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe "Conference on Security and Co-operation in Europe") in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005188_375-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005188-375">[357]</a></sup>

The Helsinki Accords, in which the Soviets promised to grant free elections in Europe, has been called a major concession to ensure peace by the Soviets. In practice, the Soviet government significantly curbed the [rule of law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rule_of_law "Rule of law"), [civil liberties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_liberties "Civil liberties"), [protection of law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Criminal_justice "Criminal justice") and [guarantees of property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_rights "Property rights"),<sup id="cite_ref-Pipes2001_376-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Pipes2001-376">[358]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pipes1994_377-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Pipes1994-377">[359]</a></sup> which were considered examples of "bourgeois morality" by Soviet legal theorists such as [Andrey Vyshinsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrey_Vyshinsky "Andrey Vyshinsky").<sup id="cite_ref-Vyshinsky1949_378-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Vyshinsky1949-378">[360]</a></sup> The Soviet Union signed legally-binding human rights documents, such as the [International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights "International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights") in 1973 and the Helsinki Accords in 1975, but they were neither widely known or accessible to people living under Communist rule, nor were they taken seriously by the Communist authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-thomas-hrideas_379-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-thomas-hrideas-379">[361]</a></sup><sup><span title="Page / location: 117">: 117 </span></sup>  Human rights activists in the Soviet Union were regularly subjected to harassment, repressions and arrests.

The pro-Soviet American business magnate [Armand Hammer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armand_Hammer "Armand Hammer") of [Occidental Petroleum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occidental_Petroleum "Occidental Petroleum") often mediated trade relations. Author [Daniel Yergin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Yergin "Daniel Yergin"), in his book _The Prize_, writes that Hammer "ended up as a go-between for five Soviet General Secretaries and seven U.S. Presidents."<sup id="cite_ref-380"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-380">[362]</a></sup> Hammer had extensive business relationship in the Soviet Union stretching back to the 1920s with Lenin's approval.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_381-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-:7-381">[363]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:8_382-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-:8-382">[364]</a></sup> According to _Christian Science Monitor_ in 1980, "although his business dealings with the Soviet Union were cut short when Stalin came to power, he had more or less single-handedly laid the groundwork for the \[1980\] state of Western trade with the Soviet Union."<sup id="cite_ref-:7_381-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-:7-381">[363]</a></sup> In 1974, Brezhnev "publicly recognized Hammer's role in facilitating East-West trade." By 1981, according to the _New York Times_ in that year, Hammer was on a "first-name basis with Leonid Brezhnev."<sup id="cite_ref-:8_382-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-:8-382">[364]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/1979_Iranian_Revolution.jpg/170px-1979_Iranian_Revolution.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1979_Iranian_Revolution.jpg)

Iranian people protesting against the [Pahlavi dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pahlavi_dynasty "Pahlavi dynasty"), during the [Iranian Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_Revolution "Iranian Revolution")

Kissinger and Nixon were "realists" who deemphasized idealistic goals like anti-communism or promotion of democracy worldwide because those goals were too expensive in terms of America's economic capabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-383"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-383">[365]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears (November 2023)">pages&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Instead of a Cold War they wanted peace, trade and cultural exchanges. They realized that Americans were no longer willing to tax themselves for idealistic foreign policy goals, especially for containment policies that never seemed to produce positive results. Instead, Nixon and Kissinger sought to downsize America's global commitments in proportion to its reduced economic, moral and political power. They rejected "idealism" as impractical and too expensive, and neither man showed much sensitivity to the plight of people living under Communism. Kissinger's realism fell out of fashion as idealism returned to American foreign policy with Carter's moralism emphasizing human rights, and Reagan's rollback strategy aimed at destroying Communism.<sup id="cite_ref-384"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-384">[366]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears (November 2023)">pages&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>

### Late 1970s deterioration of relations

In the 1970s, the KGB, led by [Yuri Andropov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Andropov "Yuri Andropov"), continued to persecute distinguished [Soviet dissidents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_dissidents "Soviet dissidents"), such as [Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn "Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn") and [Andrei Sakharov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrei_Sakharov "Andrei Sakharov"), who were criticising the Soviet leadership in harsh terms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005186_385-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005186-385">[367]</a></sup> Indirect conflict between the superpowers continued through this period of détente in the Third World, particularly during political crises in the Middle East, Chile, Ethiopia, and Angola.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005178_386-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005178-386">[368]</a></sup>

In 1973, Nixon announced his administration was committed to seeking [most favored nation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Most_favoured_nation "Most favoured nation") trade status with the USSR,<sup id="cite_ref-387"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-387">[369]</a></sup> which was challenged by Congress in the [Jackson-Vanik Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jackson%E2%80%93Vanik_amendment "Jackson–Vanik amendment").<sup id="cite_ref-388"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-388">[370]</a></sup> The United States had long linked trade with the Soviet Union to its foreign policy toward the Soviet Union and, especially since the early 1980s, to [Soviet human rights policies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights_in_the_Soviet_Union "Human rights in the Soviet Union"). The [Jackson-Vanik Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jackson-Vanik_Amendment "Jackson-Vanik Amendment"), which was attached to the [1974 Trade Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade_Act_of_1974 "Trade Act of 1974"), linked the granting of [most-favored-nation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Most-favored-nation "Most-favored-nation") to the USSR to the right of persecuted [Soviet Jews](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Jews "Soviet Jews") to emigrate. Because the Soviet Union refused the right of emigration to Jewish [refuseniks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refusenik "Refusenik"), the ability of the President to apply most-favored nation trade status to the Soviet Union was restricted.<sup id="cite_ref-389"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-389">[371]</a></sup>

Although President [Jimmy Carter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jimmy_Carter "Jimmy Carter") tried to place another limit on the arms race with a [SALT II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks#SALT_II_Treaty "Strategic Arms Limitation Talks") agreement in 1979,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBBC1979_390-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC1979-390">[372]</a></sup> his efforts were undermined by the other events that year, including the [Iranian Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iranian_Revolution "Iranian Revolution") and the [Nicaraguan Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicaraguan_Revolution "Nicaraguan Revolution"), which both ousted pro-US governments, and his retaliation against the [Soviet coup in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Storm-333 "Operation Storm-333") in December.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber1993194–197_117-12"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber1993194%E2%80%93197-117">[111]</a></sup>

## New Cold War (1979–1985)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Overzicht_op_Museumplein_met_spandoek_The_Dutch_disease_is_better_for_peace_o%2C_Bestanddeelnr_253-8627.jpg/220px-Overzicht_op_Museumplein_met_spandoek_The_Dutch_disease_is_better_for_peace_o%2C_Bestanddeelnr_253-8627.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Overzicht_op_Museumplein_met_spandoek_The_Dutch_disease_is_better_for_peace_o,_Bestanddeelnr_253-8627.jpg)

Protest in Amsterdam against the deployment of [Pershing II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pershing_II "Pershing II") missiles in Europe, 1981

The term _new Cold War_ refers to the period of intensive reawakening of Cold War tensions and conflicts in the late 1970s and early 1980s. Tensions greatly increased between the major powers with both sides becoming more militant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliday20012e_391-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliday20012e-391">[373]</a></sup> [Diggins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Patrick_Diggins "John Patrick Diggins") says, "Reagan went all out to fight the second cold war, by supporting counterinsurgencies in the third world."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiggins2007267_392-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiggins2007267-392">[374]</a></sup> [Cox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Cox_(academic) "Michael Cox (academic)") says, "The intensity of this 'second' Cold War was as great as its duration was short."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox199018_393-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox199018-393">[375]</a></sup>

### Soviet invasion of Afghanistan and end of détente

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/SovietInvasionAfghanistanMap.png/220px-SovietInvasionAfghanistanMap.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SovietInvasionAfghanistanMap.png)

The Soviet invasion during [Operation Storm-333](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Storm-333 "Operation Storm-333") on 26 December 1979

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg/220px-Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Reagan_sitting_with_people_from_the_Afghanistan-Pakistan_region_in_February_1983.jpg)

President Reagan publicizes his support by meeting with [Afghan mujahideen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_mujahideen "Afghan mujahideen") leaders in the White House, 1983.

In April 1978, the communist [People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_of_Afghanistan "People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan") (PDPA) seized power in [Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan "Afghanistan") in the [Saur Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saur_Revolution "Saur Revolution"). Within months, opponents of the communist regime launched an uprising in eastern Afghanistan that quickly expanded into a [civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_conflict "Afghan conflict") waged by guerrilla [mujahideen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mujahideen "Mujahideen") against government forces countrywide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussain2005108–109_394-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussain2005108%E2%80%93109-394">[376]</a></sup> The [Islamic Unity of Afghanistan Mujahideen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghan_mujahideen "Afghan mujahideen") insurgents received military training and weapons in neighboring [Pakistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan "Pakistan") and [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarr2004157–158_395-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarr2004157%E2%80%93158-395">[377]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsella1992_396-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsella1992-396">[378]</a></sup> while the Soviet Union sent thousands of military advisers to support the PDPA government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussain2005108–109_394-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussain2005108%E2%80%93109-394">[376]</a></sup> Meanwhile, increasing friction between the competing factions of the PDPA—the dominant [Khalq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khalq "Khalq") and the more moderate [Parcham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parcham "Parcham")—resulted in the dismissal of Parchami cabinet members and the arrest of Parchami military officers under the pretext of a Parchami coup. By mid-1979, the United States had started a covert program to assist the mujahideen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeher200468–69,_94_397-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeher200468%E2%80%9369,_94-397">[379]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-398"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-398">[380]</a></sup>

In September 1979, Khalqist President [Nur Muhammad Taraki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nur_Muhammad_Taraki "Nur Muhammad Taraki") was assassinated in a coup within the PDPA orchestrated by fellow Khalq member [Hafizullah Amin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hafizullah_Amin "Hafizullah Amin"), who assumed the presidency. Distrusted by the Soviets, Amin was assassinated by Soviet special forces during [Operation Storm-333](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Storm-333 "Operation Storm-333") in December 1979. Afghan forces suffered losses during the Soviet operation; 30 Afghan palace guards and over 300 army guards were killed while another 150 were captured.<sup id="cite_ref-399"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-399">[381]</a></sup> Two of Amin's sons, an 11-year-old and a 9-year-old, died from shrapnel wounds sustained during the clashes.<sup id="cite_ref-400"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-400">[382]</a></sup> In the aftermath of the operation, a total of 1,700 Afghan soldiers who surrendered to Soviet forces were taken as prisoners,<sup id="cite_ref-Lessons_from_last_war_401-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Lessons_from_last_war-401">[383]</a></sup> and the Soviets installed [Babrak Karmal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babrak_Karmal "Babrak Karmal"), the leader of the PDPA's Parcham faction, as Amin's successor. Veterans of the Soviet Union's [Alpha Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_Group "Alpha Group") have stated that Operation Storm-333 was one of the most successful in the unit's history. Documents released following the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union") in the 1990s revealed that the Soviet leadership believed Amin had secret contacts within the [American embassy in Kabul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embassy_of_the_United_States,_Kabul "Embassy of the United States, Kabul") and "was capable of reaching an agreement with the United States";<sup id="cite_ref-402"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-402">[384]</a></sup> however, allegations of Amin colluding with the Americans have been widely discredited.<sup id="cite_ref-403"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-403">[385]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-404"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-404">[386]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-405"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-405">[387]</a></sup> The PDBA was tasked to fill the vacuum and carried out a purge of Amin supporters. Soviet troops were deployed to put Afghanistan under Soviet control with Karmal in more substantial numbers, although the Soviet government did not expect to do most of the fighting in Afghanistan. As a result, however, the Soviets were now directly involved in what had been a domestic war in Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKalinovsky201125–28_406-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKalinovsky201125%E2%80%9328-406">[388]</a></sup>

Carter responded to the Soviet invasion by withdrawing the [SALT II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks#SALT_II_Treaty "Strategic Arms Limitation Talks") treaty from ratification, imposing embargoes on grain and technology shipments to the USSR, and demanding a significant increase in military spending, and further announced the [boycott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics_boycott "1980 Summer Olympics boycott") of the [1980 Summer Olympics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics "1980 Summer Olympics") in Moscow, which was joined by 65 other nations.<sup id="cite_ref-407"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-407">[389]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-408"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-408">[390]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-409"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-409">[391]</a></sup> He described the Soviet incursion as "the most serious threat to the peace since the Second World War".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005211_410-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005211-410">[392]</a></sup>

### Reagan and Thatcher

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/President_Ronald_Reagan_with_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_During_a_Working_Luncheon_at_Camp_David_%28retouched%29.jpg/220px-President_Ronald_Reagan_with_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_During_a_Working_Luncheon_at_Camp_David_%28retouched%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:President_Ronald_Reagan_with_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_During_a_Working_Luncheon_at_Camp_David_(retouched).jpg)

President Reagan with Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher during a working luncheon at [Camp David](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Camp_David "Camp David"), December 1984

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Cold_War_Map_1980.svg/280px-Cold_War_Map_1980.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Cold_War_Map_1980.svg)

The world map of military alliances in 1980

In January 1977, four years prior to becoming president, [Ronald Reagan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Reagan "Ronald Reagan") bluntly stated, in a conversation with [Richard V. Allen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_V._Allen "Richard V. Allen"), his basic expectation in relation to the Cold War. "My idea of American policy toward the Soviet Union is simple, and some would say simplistic," he said. "It is this: We win and they lose. What do you think of that?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2000_411-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2000-411">[393]</a></sup> In 1980, Ronald Reagan defeated Jimmy Carter in the [1980 presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1980_United_States_presidential_election "1980 United States presidential election"), vowing to increase military spending and confront the Soviets everywhere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005189_412-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005189-412">[394]</a></sup> Both Reagan and new British Prime Minister [Margaret Thatcher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher "Margaret Thatcher") denounced the Soviet Union and its ideology. Reagan labeled the Soviet Union an "[evil empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evil_Empire_speech "Evil Empire speech")" and predicted that Communism would be left on the "[ash heap of history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ash_heap_of_history "Ash heap of history")," while Thatcher inculpated the Soviets as "bent on world dominance."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005197_413-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005197-413">[395]</a></sup> In 1982, Reagan tried to cut off Moscow's access to hard currency by impeding its proposed gas line to Western Europe. It hurt the Soviet economy, but it also caused ill will among American allies in Europe who counted on that revenue. Reagan retreated on this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEsno2018281–304_414-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEsno2018281%E2%80%93304-414">[396]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraebnerBurnsSiracusa200829–31_415-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraebnerBurnsSiracusa200829%E2%80%9331-415">[397]</a></sup>

By early 1985, Reagan's anti-communist position had developed into a stance known as the new [Reagan Doctrine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reagan_Doctrine "Reagan Doctrine")—which, in addition to containment, formulated an additional right to subvert existing communist governments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraebnerBurnsSiracusa200876_416-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraebnerBurnsSiracusa200876-416">[398]</a></sup> Besides continuing Carter's policy of supporting the Islamic opponents of the Soviet Union and the Soviet-backed [PDPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_of_Afghanistan "People's Democratic Party of Afghanistan") government in Afghanistan, the CIA also sought to weaken the Soviet Union itself by promoting [Islamism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islamism "Islamism") in the majority-Muslim [Central Asian Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Central_Asia "Soviet Central Asia").<sup id="cite_ref-Singh_417-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Singh-417">[399]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Harvard_citation_documentation#Wikilink_to_citation_does_not_work" title="Template:Harvard citation documentation"><span title="Template:Harvard citation documentation#Wikilink to citation does not work">citation not found</span></a></i>]</sup> Additionally, the CIA encouraged anti-communist Pakistan's ISI to train Muslims from around the world to participate in the [jihad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jihad "Jihad") against the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-Singh_417-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Singh-417">[399]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Harvard_citation_documentation#Wikilink_to_citation_does_not_work" title="Template:Harvard citation documentation"><span title="Template:Harvard citation documentation#Wikilink to citation does not work">citation not found</span></a></i>]</sup>

### Polish Solidarity movement and martial law

[Pope John Paul II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II "Pope John Paul II") provided a moral focus for [anti-communism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-communism "Anti-communism"); a visit to his native Poland in 1979 stimulated a religious and nationalist resurgence centered on the [Solidarity movement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union) "Solidarity (Polish trade union)") trade union that galvanized opposition and may have led to his [attempted assassination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attempted_assassination_of_Pope_John_Paul_II "Attempted assassination of Pope John Paul II") two years later.<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In December 1981, Poland's [Wojciech Jaruzelski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wojciech_Jaruzelski "Wojciech Jaruzelski") reacted to the crisis by imposing [a period of martial law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martial_law_in_Poland "Martial law in Poland"). Reagan imposed economic sanctions on Poland in response.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005219–222_418-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005219%E2%80%93222-418">[400]</a></sup> [Mikhail Suslov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Suslov "Mikhail Suslov"), the Kremlin's top ideologist, advised Soviet leaders not to intervene if Poland fell under the control of Solidarity, for fear it might lead to heavy economic sanctions, resulting in a catastrophe for the Soviet economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005219–222_418-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005219%E2%80%93222-418">[400]</a></sup>

### US and USSR military and economic issues

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/220px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg)

US and USSR/Russian nuclear weapons stockpiles, 1945–2006

The Soviet Union had built up a military that consumed as much as 25 percent of its gross national product at the expense of [consumer goods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consumer_goods_in_the_Soviet_Union "Consumer goods in the Soviet Union") and investment in civilian sectors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002332_419-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002332-419">[401]</a></sup> Soviet spending on the [arms race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_race "Arms race") and other Cold War commitments both caused and exacerbated deep-seated structural problems in the Soviet system,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETowle159_420-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTETowle159-420">[402]</a></sup> which experienced at least [a decade of economic stagnation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Era_of_Stagnation "Era of Stagnation") during the late Brezhnev years.

Soviet investment in the defense sector was not driven by military necessity but in large part by the interests of the [nomenklatura](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nomenklatura "Nomenklatura"), which was dependent on the sector for their own power and privileges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002335_421-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002335-421">[403]</a></sup> The [Soviet Armed Forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Armed_Forces "Soviet Armed Forces") became the largest in the world in terms of the numbers and types of weapons they possessed, in the number of troops in their ranks, and in the sheer size of their [military–industrial base](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military%E2%80%93industrial_complex "Military–industrial complex").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOdom20001_422-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOdom20001-422">[404]</a></sup> However, the quantitative advantages held by the Soviet military often concealed areas where the Eastern Bloc dramatically lagged behind the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002340_423-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002340-423">[405]</a></sup> For example, the [Persian Gulf War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_War "Gulf War") demonstrated how the [armor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vehicle_armour "Vehicle armour"), [fire control systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fire-control_system "Fire-control system"), and firing range of the Soviet Union's most common main battle tank, the [T-72](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T-72 "T-72"), were drastically inferior to the American [M1 Abrams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M1_Abrams "M1 Abrams"), yet the USSR fielded almost three times as many T-72s as the US deployed M1s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans1992_424-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans1992-424">[406]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/SDIO_Delta_Star.jpg/170px-SDIO_Delta_Star.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SDIO_Delta_Star.jpg)

Delta 183 launch vehicle lifts off, carrying the [Strategic Defense Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Defense_Initiative "Strategic Defense Initiative") sensor experiment "Delta Star".

By the early 1980s, the USSR had built up a military arsenal and army surpassing that of the United States. Soon after the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan, President Carter began massively building up the United States military. This buildup was accelerated by the Reagan administration, which increased the military spending from 5.3 percent of GNP in 1981 to 6.5 percent in 1986,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarlinerAlesina19916_425-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarlinerAlesina19916-425">[407]</a></sup> the largest peacetime defense buildup in United States history.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeeney2006_426-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeeney2006-426">[408]</a></sup> The American-Soviet tensions present during 1983 was defined by some as the start of "Cold War II". Whilst in retrospective this phase of the Cold War was generally defined as a "war of words",<sup id="cite_ref-427"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-427">[409]</a></sup> the Soviet's "peace offensive" was largely rejected by the West.<sup id="cite_ref-428"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-428">[410]</a></sup>

Tensions continued to intensify as Reagan revived the [B-1 Lancer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rockwell_B-1_Lancer "Rockwell B-1 Lancer") program, which had been canceled by the Carter administration, produced [LGM-118 Peacekeeper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LGM-118_Peacekeeper "LGM-118 Peacekeeper") missiles,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFederation_of_American_Scientists2000_429-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFederation_of_American_Scientists2000-429">[411]</a></sup> installed US cruise missiles in Europe, and announced the experimental [Strategic Defense Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Defense_Initiative "Strategic Defense Initiative"), dubbed "Star Wars" by the media, a defense program to shoot down missiles in mid-flight.<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Soviets deployed [RSD-10 Pioneer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RSD-10_Pioneer "RSD-10 Pioneer") [ballistic missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ballistic_missile "Ballistic missile") targeting Western Europe, and NATO decided, under the impetus of the Carter presidency, to deploy [MGM-31 Pershing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MGM-31_Pershing "MGM-31 Pershing") and cruise missiles in Europe, primarily West Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005202_430-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005202-430">[412]</a></sup> This deployment placed missiles just 10 minutes' striking distance from Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarthoff1994881–882_431-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarthoff1994881%E2%80%93882-431">[413]</a></sup>

After Reagan's military buildup, the Soviet Union did not respond by further building its military,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELebowStein1994_432-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELebowStein1994-432">[414]</a></sup> because the enormous military expenses, along with inefficient [planned manufacturing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planned_economy "Planned economy") and [collectivized agriculture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collectivization_in_the_Soviet_Union "Collectivization in the Soviet Union"), were already a heavy burden for the [Soviet economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_the_Soviet_Union "Economy of the Soviet Union").<sup id="cite_ref-433"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-433">[415]</a></sup> At the same time, [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia") increased oil production,<sup id="cite_ref-Gaidar_434-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Gaidar-434">[416]</a></sup> even as other non-OPEC nations were increasing production.<sup id="cite_ref-435"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-435">[S]</a></sup> These developments contributed to the [1980s oil glut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1980s_oil_glut "1980s oil glut"), which affected the Soviet Union as oil was the main source of Soviet export revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002332_419-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002332-419">[401]</a></sup> Issues with [command economics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planned_economy#Command_economy "Planned economy"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardtKaufman19951_436-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardtKaufman19951-436">[417]</a></sup> oil price decreases and large military expenditures gradually brought the Soviet economy to stagnation.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaidar_434-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Gaidar-434">[416]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/1985_CPA_5685.jpg/170px-1985_CPA_5685.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1985_CPA_5685.jpg)

After ten-year-old American [Samantha Smith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samantha_Smith "Samantha Smith") wrote a letter to [Yuri Andropov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Andropov "Yuri Andropov") expressing her fear of nuclear war, Andropov invited Smith to the Soviet Union.

On 1 September 1983, the Soviet Union shot down [Korean Air Lines Flight 007](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korean_Air_Lines_Flight_007 "Korean Air Lines Flight 007"), a [Boeing 747](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_747 "Boeing 747") with 269 people aboard, including sitting Congressman [Larry McDonald](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larry_McDonald "Larry McDonald"), an action which Reagan characterized as a massacre. The airliner was en route from Anchorage to Seoul but owing to a navigational mistake made by the crew, it drifted from its original planned route and flew through Russian [prohibited airspace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prohibited_airspace "Prohibited airspace") past the west coast of [Sakhalin Island](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sakhalin "Sakhalin") near [Moneron Island](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moneron_Island "Moneron Island"). The [Soviet Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Air_Force "Soviet Air Force") treated the unidentified aircraft as an intruding U.S. [spy plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surveillance_aircraft "Surveillance aircraft") and destroyed it with [air-to-air missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air-to-air_missiles "Air-to-air missiles"). The Soviet Union found the wreckage under the sea two weeks later on 15 September and found the [flight recorders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flight_recorder "Flight recorder") in October, but this information was kept secret by the Soviet authorities until after the [country's collapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union").<sup id="cite_ref-tapes_437-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-tapes-437">[418]</a></sup> The incident increased support for military deployment, overseen by Reagan, which stood in place until the later accords between Reagan and Mikhail Gorbachev.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbottHannifinMagnusonDoerner1983_438-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbottHannifinMagnusonDoerner1983-438">[419]</a></sup> During the early hours of 26 September 1983, the [1983 Soviet nuclear false alarm incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1983_Soviet_nuclear_false_alarm_incident "1983 Soviet nuclear false alarm incident") occurred; systems in [Serpukhov-15](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serpukhov-15 "Serpukhov-15") underwent a glitch that claimed several [intercontinental ballistic missiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile "Intercontinental ballistic missile") were heading towards Russia, but officer [Stanislav Petrov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanislav_Petrov "Stanislav Petrov") correctly suspected it was a [false alarm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/False_alarm "False alarm"), ensuring the Soviets did not respond to the non-existent attack.<sup id="cite_ref-439"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-439">[420]</a></sup> As such, he has been credited as "the man who saved the world".<sup id="cite_ref-440"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-440">[421]</a></sup> The [Able Archer 83](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Able_Archer_83 "Able Archer 83") exercise in November 1983, a realistic simulation of a coordinated NATO nuclear release, was perhaps the most dangerous moment since the Cuban Missile Crisis, as the Soviet leadership feared that a nuclear attack might be imminent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005228_441-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005228-441">[422]</a></sup>

American domestic public concerns about intervening in foreign conflicts persisted from the end of the Vietnam War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002323_442-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002323-442">[423]</a></sup> The Reagan administration emphasized the use of quick, low-cost [counterinsurgency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Counterinsurgency "Counterinsurgency") tactics to intervene in foreign conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002323_442-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002323-442">[423]</a></sup> In 1983, the Reagan administration intervened in the multisided [Lebanese Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War "Lebanese Civil War"), [invaded Grenada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_invasion_of_Grenada "United States invasion of Grenada"), [bombed Libya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1986_United_States_bombing_of_Libya "1986 United States bombing of Libya") and backed the Central American [Contras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contras "Contras"), anti-communist paramilitaries seeking to overthrow the Soviet-aligned [Sandinista](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sandinista_National_Liberation_Front "Sandinista National Liberation Front") government in Nicaragua.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005212_188-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005212-188">[182]</a></sup> While Reagan's interventions against Grenada and Libya were popular in the United States, his backing of the Contra rebels was [mired in controversy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Contra_affair "Iran–Contra affair").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReagan1991_443-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReagan1991-443">[424]</a></sup> The Reagan administration's backing of the military government of [Guatemala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guatemala "Guatemala") during the [Guatemalan Civil War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guatemalan_Civil_War "Guatemalan Civil War"), in particular the regime of [Efraín Ríos Montt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Efra%C3%ADn_R%C3%ADos_Montt "Efraín Ríos Montt"), was also controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENew_York_Times2013_444-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTENew_York_Times2013-444">[425]</a></sup>

Meanwhile, the Soviets incurred high costs for their own foreign interventions. Although Brezhnev was convinced in 1979 that the [Soviet war in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War "Soviet–Afghan War") would be brief, Muslim guerrillas, aided by the US, China, Britain, Saudi Arabia and Pakistan,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsella1992_396-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsella1992-396">[378]</a></sup> waged a fierce resistance against the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002314_445-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002314-445">[426]</a></sup> The Kremlin sent nearly 100,000 troops to support its puppet regime in Afghanistan, leading many outside observers to dub the war "the Soviets' Vietnam".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002314_445-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002314-445">[426]</a></sup> However, Moscow's quagmire in Afghanistan was far more disastrous for the Soviets than Vietnam had been for the Americans because the conflict coincided with a period of internal decay and domestic crisis in the Soviet system.

A senior [US State Department](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State "United States Department of State") official predicted such an outcome as early as 1980, positing that the invasion resulted in part from a:

> ...domestic crisis within the Soviet system. ... It may be that the thermodynamic law of [entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entropy "Entropy") has ... caught up with the Soviet system, which now seems to expend more energy on simply maintaining its equilibrium than on improving itself. We could be seeing a period of foreign movement at a time of internal decay.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDobrynin2001438–439_446-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDobrynin2001438%E2%80%93439-446">[427]</a></sup>

## Final years (1985–1991)

### Gorbachev's reforms

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/President_Ronald_Reagan_and_Soviet_General_Secretary_Mikhail_Gorbachev_at_the_first_Summit_in_Geneva%2C_Switzerland.jpg/220px-President_Ronald_Reagan_and_Soviet_General_Secretary_Mikhail_Gorbachev_at_the_first_Summit_in_Geneva%2C_Switzerland.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:President_Ronald_Reagan_and_Soviet_General_Secretary_Mikhail_Gorbachev_at_the_first_Summit_in_Geneva,_Switzerland.jpg)

[Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev") in one-to-one discussions with US President [Ronald Reagan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ronald_Reagan "Ronald Reagan")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Reagan_and_Gorbachev_signing.jpg/220px-Reagan_and_Gorbachev_signing.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Reagan_and_Gorbachev_signing.jpg)

Mikhail Gorbachev and Ronald Reagan sign the [INF Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intermediate-Range_Nuclear_Forces_Treaty "Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty") at the White House, 1987.

By the time the comparatively youthful [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev") became [General Secretary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "General Secretary of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union") in 1985,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005197_413-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005197-413">[395]</a></sup> the Soviet economy was stagnant and faced a sharp fall in foreign currency earnings as a result of the downward slide in oil prices in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002331–333_447-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002331%E2%80%93333-447">[428]</a></sup> These issues prompted Gorbachev to investigate measures to revive the ailing state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002331–333_447-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002331%E2%80%93333-447">[428]</a></sup>

An ineffectual start led to the conclusion that deeper structural changes were necessary, and in June 1987 Gorbachev announced an agenda of economic reform called _[perestroika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perestroika "Perestroika")_, or restructuring.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005231–233_448-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005231%E2%80%93233-448">[429]</a></sup> Perestroika relaxed the [production quota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Production_quota "Production quota") system, allowed cooperative ownership of small businesses and paved the way for foreign investment. These measures were intended to redirect the country's resources from costly Cold War military commitments to more productive areas in the civilian sector.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005231–233_448-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005231%E2%80%93233-448">[429]</a></sup>

Despite initial skepticism in the West, the new Soviet leader proved to be committed to reversing the Soviet Union's deteriorating economic condition instead of continuing the arms race with the West.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002300–340_449-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber2002300%E2%80%93340-449">[430]</a></sup> Partly as a way to fight off internal opposition from party cliques to his reforms, Gorbachev simultaneously introduced _[glasnost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasnost "Glasnost")_, or openness, which increased freedom of the press and the transparency of state institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibbs19997_450-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibbs19997-450">[431]</a></sup> _Glasnost_ was intended to reduce the corruption at the top of the [Communist Party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "Communist Party of the Soviet Union") and moderate the [abuse of power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abuse_of_power "Abuse of power") in the [Central Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Committee_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibbs199933_451-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibbs199933-451">[432]</a></sup> Glasnost also enabled increased contact between Soviet citizens and the Western world, particularly with the United States, contributing to the accelerating [détente](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D%C3%A9tente "Détente") between the two nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibbs199961_452-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibbs199961-452">[433]</a></sup>

### Thaw in relations

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Bush_Gorba_P15623-25A.jpg/220px-Bush_Gorba_P15623-25A.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bush_Gorba_P15623-25A.jpg)

The beginning of the 1990s brought a thaw in relations between the superpowers.

In response to the Kremlin's military and [political concessions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concession_(politics) "Concession (politics)"), Reagan agreed to renew talks on economic issues and the scaling-back of the arms race.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005229–230_453-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005229%E2%80%93230-453">[434]</a></sup> The first [summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geneva_Summit_(1985) "Geneva Summit (1985)") was held in November 1985 in [Geneva](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geneva "Geneva"), [Switzerland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Switzerland "Switzerland").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005229–230_453-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005229%E2%80%93230-453">[434]</a></sup> At one stage the two men, accompanied only by an interpreter, agreed in principle to reduce each country's nuclear arsenal by 50 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-454"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-454">[435]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Harvard_citation_documentation#Wikilink_to_citation_does_not_work" title="Template:Harvard citation documentation"><span title="Template:Harvard citation documentation#Wikilink to citation does not work">citation not found</span></a></i>]</sup> A [second summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk_Summit "Reykjavík Summit") was held in October 1986 in [Reykjavík](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reykjav%C3%ADk "Reykjavík"), [Iceland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iceland "Iceland"). Talks went well until the focus shifted to Reagan's proposed [Strategic Defense Initiative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strategic_Defense_Initiative "Strategic Defense Initiative") (SDI), which Gorbachev wanted to be eliminated. Reagan refused.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENew_York_Times1988_455-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTENew_York_Times1988-455">[436]</a></sup> The negotiations failed, but the third summit ([Washington Summit (1987)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_Summit_(1987) "Washington Summit (1987)"), 8–10 December 1987) led to a breakthrough with the signing of the [Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intermediate-Range_Nuclear_Forces_Treaty "Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty") (INF). The INF treaty eliminated all nuclear-armed, ground-launched ballistic and cruise missiles with ranges between 500 and 5,500 kilometers (310 and 3,420 mi) and their infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFederation_of_American_Scientists_456-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFederation_of_American_Scientists-456">[437]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/President_Ronald_Reagan_making_his_Berlin_Wall_speech.jpg/220px-President_Ronald_Reagan_making_his_Berlin_Wall_speech.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:President_Ronald_Reagan_making_his_Berlin_Wall_speech.jpg)

"[Tear down this wall!](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tear_down_this_wall! "Tear down this wall!")" speech: Reagan speaking in front of the [Brandenburg Gate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brandenburg_Gate "Brandenburg Gate"), 12 June 1987

During 1988, it became apparent to the Soviets that oil and gas subsidies, along with the cost of maintaining massive troops levels, represented a substantial economic drain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShearman199576_457-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShearman199576-457">[438]</a></sup> In addition, the security advantage of [a buffer zone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Europe "Central Europe") was recognised as irrelevant and the Soviets [officially declared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinatra_Doctrine "Sinatra Doctrine") that they would no longer intervene in the affairs of [satellite states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warsaw_Pact "Warsaw Pact") in Central and Eastern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005248_458-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005248-458">[439]</a></sup> [George H. W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_H._W._Bush "George H. W. Bush") and Gorbachev met at the [Moscow Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moscow_Summit_(1988) "Moscow Summit (1988)") in May 1988 and the [Governors Island Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Governors_Island_Summit "Governors Island Summit") in December 1988.

In 1989, [Soviet forces withdrew from Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_withdrawal_from_Afghanistan "Soviet withdrawal from Afghanistan") without achieving their objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005235–236_459-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005235%E2%80%93236-459">[440]</a></sup> Later that year, the [Berlin Wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall "Fall of the Berlin Wall"), the [Inner German border](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_German_border#Fall_of_the_inner_German_border "Inner German border") and the [Iron Curtain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron_Curtain#Fall_of_the_Iron_Curtain "Iron Curtain") fell. On 3 December 1989, Gorbachev and Bush declared the Cold War over at the [Malta Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malta_Summit "Malta Summit"). In February 1990, Gorbachev agreed with the US-proposed [Treaty on the Final Settlement with Respect to Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Final_Settlement_with_Respect_to_Germany "Treaty on the Final Settlement with Respect to Germany") and signed it on 12 September 1990, paving the way for the [German reunification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_reunification "German reunification").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShearman199576_457-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShearman199576-457">[438]</a></sup> When the Berlin Wall came down, Gorbachev's "[Common European Home](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_European_Home "Common European Home")" concept began to take shape.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Navigator1989_460-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Navigator1989-460">[441]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBBC1989_461-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBBC1989-461">[442]</a></sup> The two former adversaries were partners in the [Gulf War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gulf_War "Gulf War") against [Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq "Ba'athist Iraq") (August 1990 – February 1991).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENewman199341_462-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewman199341-462">[443]</a></sup> During the final summit in Moscow in July 1991, Gorbachev and Bush signed the [START I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_I "START I") arms control treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005255_463-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005255-463">[444]</a></sup>

### Eastern Europe breaks away

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Oliver_Mark_-_Otto_Habsburg-Lothringen%2C_P%C3%B6cking_2006.jpg/150px-Oliver_Mark_-_Otto_Habsburg-Lothringen%2C_P%C3%B6cking_2006.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Oliver_Mark_-_Otto_Habsburg-Lothringen,_P%C3%B6cking_2006.jpg)

[Otto von Habsburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otto_von_Habsburg "Otto von Habsburg"), who played a leading role in opening the Iron Curtain

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R0518-182%2C_Erich_Honecker.jpg/150px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R0518-182%2C_Erich_Honecker.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-R0518-182,_Erich_Honecker.jpg)

East German leader [Erich Honecker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erich_Honecker "Erich Honecker") lost control in August 1989.

Two developments dominated the decade that followed: the increasingly apparent crumbling of the Soviet Union's economic and political structures, and the patchwork attempts at reforms to reverse that process. Kenneth S. Deffeyes argued in _[Beyond Oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beyond_Oil "Beyond Oil")_ that the [Reagan administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Ronald_Reagan "Presidency of Ronald Reagan") encouraged [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia") to [lower the price of oil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1980s_oil_glut "1980s oil glut") to the point where the Soviets could not make a profit selling their oil, and resulted in the depletion of the country's [hard currency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hard_currency "Hard currency") reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-464"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-464">[445]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/00_P%C3%A1neur%C3%B3pai_Piknik_eml%C3%A9khely.jpg/150px-00_P%C3%A1neur%C3%B3pai_Piknik_eml%C3%A9khely.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:00_P%C3%A1neur%C3%B3pai_Piknik_eml%C3%A9khely.jpg)

The [Pan-European Picnic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-European_Picnic "Pan-European Picnic") took place in August 1989 on the Hungarian-Austrian border.

Brezhnev's next two successors, transitional figures with deep roots in his tradition, did not last long. [Yuri Andropov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Andropov "Yuri Andropov") was 68 years old and [Konstantin Chernenko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Chernenko "Konstantin Chernenko") 72 when they assumed power; both died in less than two years. In an attempt to avoid a third short-lived leader, in 1985, the Soviets turned to the next generation and selected [Mikhail Gorbachev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev "Mikhail Gorbachev"). He made significant changes in the economy and party leadership, called _[perestroika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perestroika "Perestroika")_. His policy of _[glasnost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasnost "Glasnost")_ freed public [access to information](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_of_information "Freedom of information") after decades of heavy government censorship. Gorbachev also moved to end the Cold War. In 1988, the USSR abandoned its [war in Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Afghan_War "Soviet–Afghan War") and began to [withdraw its forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_withdrawal_from_Afghanistan "Soviet withdrawal from Afghanistan"). In the following year, [Gorbachev refused to interfere in the internal affairs of the Soviet satellite states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinatra_Doctrine "Sinatra Doctrine"), which paved the way for the [Revolutions of 1989](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989 "Revolutions of 1989"). In particular, the standstill of the Soviet Union at the [Pan-European Picnic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan-European_Picnic "Pan-European Picnic") in August 1989 then set a peaceful chain reaction in motion, at the end of which the Eastern Bloc collapsed. With the tearing down of the [Berlin Wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall "Fall of the Berlin Wall") and with East and West Germany pursuing re-unification, the [Iron Curtain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iron_Curtain "Iron Curtain") between [the West](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_world "Western world") and Soviet-occupied regions came down.<sup id="cite_ref-Andreas_Rödder_2009_465-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Andreas_R%C3%B6dder_2009-465">[446]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_Roser_2018_466-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Thomas_Roser_2018-466">[447]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Otmar_Lahodynsky_2014_467-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Otmar_Lahodynsky_2014-467">[448]</a></sup>

By 1989, the Soviet alliance system was on the brink of collapse, and, deprived of Soviet military support, the communist leaders of the Warsaw Pact states were losing power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005235–236_459-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005235%E2%80%93236-459">[440]</a></sup> Grassroots organizations, such as Poland's [Solidarity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union) "Solidarity (Polish trade union)") movement, rapidly gained ground with strong popular bases.

The Pan-European Picnic in August 1989 in Hungary finally started a peaceful movement that the rulers in the Eastern Bloc could not stop. It was the largest movement of refugees from East Germany since the Berlin Wall was built in 1961 and ultimately brought about the fall of the Iron Curtain. The patrons of the picnic, [Otto von Habsburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otto_von_Habsburg "Otto von Habsburg") and the Hungarian Minister of State [Imre Pozsgay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imre_Pozsgay "Imre Pozsgay"), saw the planned event as an opportunity to test Mikhail Gorbachev's reaction. The Austrian branch of the [Paneuropean Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paneuropean_Union "Paneuropean Union"), which was then headed by [Karl von Habsburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_von_Habsburg "Karl von Habsburg"), distributed thousands of brochures inviting the GDR holidaymakers in Hungary to a picnic near the border at Sopron. But with the mass exodus at the Pan-European Picnic the subsequent hesitant behavior of the ruling Socialist Unity Party of East Germany and the non-interference of the Soviet Union broke the dams. Now tens of thousands of media-informed East Germans made their way to Hungary, which was no longer willing to keep its borders completely closed or to oblige its border troops to use armed force. On the one hand, this caused disagreement among the Eastern European states and, on the other hand, it was clear to the Eastern European population that the governments no longer had absolute power.<sup id="cite_ref-Andreas_Rödder_2009_465-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Andreas_R%C3%B6dder_2009-465">[446]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_Roser_2018_466-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Thomas_Roser_2018-466">[447]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Otmar_Lahodynsky_2014_467-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Otmar_Lahodynsky_2014-467">[448]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-468"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-468">[449]</a></sup>

In 1989, the communist governments in Poland and Hungary became the first to negotiate the organization of competitive elections. In Czechoslovakia and East Germany, mass protests unseated entrenched communist leaders. The communist regimes in Bulgaria and Romania also crumbled, in the latter case as the result of a [violent uprising](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Romanian_Revolution "Romanian Revolution"). Attitudes had changed enough that US Secretary of State [James Baker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Baker "James Baker") suggested that the American government would not be opposed to Soviet intervention in Romania, on behalf of the opposition, to prevent bloodshed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarthoff1994_469-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarthoff1994-469">[450]</a></sup>

The tidal wave of change culminated with the [fall of the Berlin Wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fall_of_the_Berlin_Wall "Fall of the Berlin Wall") in November 1989, which symbolized the collapse of European communist governments and graphically ended the Iron Curtain divide of Europe. The [1989 revolutionary wave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989 "Revolutions of 1989") swept across Central and Eastern Europe and peacefully overthrew all of the Soviet-style [Marxist–Leninist states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_state "Communist state"): East Germany, Poland, Hungary, Czechoslovakia and Bulgaria;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefeberFitzmauriceVierdag1991221_470-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefeberFitzmauriceVierdag1991221-470">[451]</a></sup> Romania was the only Eastern-bloc country to topple its communist regime violently and execute its head of state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005247_471-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005247-471">[452]</a></sup>

### Soviet dissolution

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/August_1991_coup_-_awaiting_the_counterattack_outside_the_White_House_Moscow_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-August_1991_coup_-_awaiting_the_counterattack_outside_the_White_House_Moscow_-_panoramio.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:August_1991_coup_-_awaiting_the_counterattack_outside_the_White_House_Moscow_-_panoramio.jpg)

[August Coup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1991_Soviet_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt "1991 Soviet coup d'état attempt") in [Moscow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moscow "Moscow"), 1991

At the same time, the Soviet republics started legal moves towards potentially declaring [sovereignty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereignty "Sovereignty") over their territories, citing the freedom to secede in Article 72 of the USSR constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-472"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-472">[453]</a></sup> On 7 April 1990, a law was passed allowing a republic to secede if more than two-thirds of its residents voted for it in a referendum.<sup id="cite_ref-473"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-473">[454]</a></sup> Many held their first free elections in the Soviet era for their own national legislatures in 1990. Many of these legislatures proceeded to produce legislation contradicting the Union laws in what was known as the '[War of Laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_of_Laws "War of Laws")'. In 1989, the [Russian SFSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic "Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic") convened a newly elected Congress of People's Deputies. [Boris Yeltsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin "Boris Yeltsin") was elected its chairman. On 12 June 1990, the Congress [declared Russia's sovereignty over its territory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Declaration_of_State_Sovereignty_of_the_Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic "Declaration of State Sovereignty of the Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic") and proceeded to pass laws that attempted to supersede some of the Soviet laws. After a landslide victory of [Sąjūdis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S%C4%85j%C5%ABdis "Sąjūdis") in Lithuania, that country declared its independence restored on 11 March 1990, citing the illegality of the [Soviet occupation of the Baltic states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Baltic_states "Occupation of the Baltic states"). Soviet forces attempted to halt the secession by crushing popular demonstrations in Lithuania ([Bloody Sunday](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/January_Events "January Events")) and Latvia ([The Barricades](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Barricades "The Barricades")), as a result, numerous civilians were killed or wounded. However, these actions only bolstered international support for the secessionists.<sup id="cite_ref-474"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-474">[455]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Image0_ST.jpg/220px-Image0_ST.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Image0_ST.jpg)

[T-80](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/T-80 "T-80") tank on [Red Square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red_Square "Red Square") during the [August Coup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1991_Soviet_coup_attempt "1991 Soviet coup attempt")

A [referendum for the preservation of the USSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1991_Soviet_Union_referendum "1991 Soviet Union referendum") was held on 17 March 1991 in nine republics (the remainder having boycotted the vote), with the majority of the population in those republics voting for preservation of the Union in the form of a new federation. The referendum gave Gorbachev a minor boost. In the summer of 1991, the [New Union Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Union_Treaty "New Union Treaty"), which would have turned the country into a much looser Union, was agreed upon by eight republics. The signing of the treaty, however, was interrupted by the [August Coup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1991_Soviet_coup_attempt "1991 Soviet coup attempt")—an attempted coup d'état by hardline members of the government and the KGB who sought to reverse Gorbachev's reforms and reassert the central government's control over the republics. After the coup collapsed, Russian president Yeltsin was seen as a hero for his decisive actions, while Gorbachev's power was effectively ended. The balance of power tipped significantly towards the republics. In August 1991, Latvia and Estonia immediately declared the restoration of their full independence (following Lithuania's 1990 example). Gorbachev resigned as general secretary in late August, and soon afterwards, the party's activities were indefinitely suspended—effectively ending its rule. By the fall, Gorbachev could no longer influence events outside Moscow, and he was being challenged even there by Yeltsin, who had been elected [President of Russia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Russia "President of Russia") in July 1991.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Baltsk%C3%BD%C5%98et%C4%9Bz.jpg/220px-Baltsk%C3%BD%C5%98et%C4%9Bz.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Baltsk%C3%BD%C5%98et%C4%9Bz.jpg)

The human chain in [Lithuania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithuania "Lithuania") during the [Baltic Way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltic_Way "Baltic Way"), 23 August 1989

Later in August, Gorbachev resigned as [general secretary of the Communist party](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "General Secretary of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union"), and [Russian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic "Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic") President Boris Yeltsin ordered the seizure of Soviet property. Gorbachev clung to power as the President of the Soviet Union until 25 December 1991, when the [USSR dissolved](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union").<sup id="cite_ref-475"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-475">[456]</a></sup> [Fifteen states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-Soviet_states "Post-Soviet states") emerged from the Soviet Union, with by far the largest and most populous one (which also was the founder of the Soviet state with the [October Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/October_Revolution "October Revolution") in Petrograd), the [Russian Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia "Russia"), taking full responsibility for all the rights and obligations of the USSR under the Charter of the United Nations, including the financial obligations. As such, Russia assumed the Soviet Union's [UN membership and permanent membership on the Security Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia_and_the_United_Nations "Russia and the United Nations"), nuclear stockpile and the control over the armed forces; Soviet embassies abroad became Russian embassies.<sup id="cite_ref-web.archive.org_16-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-web.archive.org-16">[13]</a></sup>

In his [1992 State of the Union Address](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1992_State_of_the_Union_Address "1992 State of the Union Address"), US President George H. W. Bush expressed his emotions: "The biggest thing that has happened in the world in my life, in our lives, is this: By the grace of God, America won the Cold War."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmbroseBrinkley2011xvi_476-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmbroseBrinkley2011xvi-476">[457]</a></sup> Bush and Yeltsin met in February 1992, declaring a new era of "friendship and partnership".<sup id="cite_ref-477"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-477">[458]</a></sup> In January 1993, Bush and Yeltsin agreed to [START II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/START_II "START II"), which provided for further nuclear arms reductions on top of the original START treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-478"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-478">[459]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/CCCP_1991_%284377719733%29.jpg/220px-CCCP_1991_%284377719733%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:CCCP_1991_(4377719733).jpg)

The first [Russian McDonald's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McDonald%27s_in_Russia "McDonald's in Russia") on [Moscow's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moscow "Moscow") [Pushkin Square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pushkinskaya_Square "Pushkinskaya Square"), pictured in 1991

## Aftermath

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Cold_War_border_changes.png/260px-Cold_War_border_changes.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Cold_War_border_changes.png)

Changes in national boundaries after the end of the Cold War

In summing up the international ramifications of these events, [Vladislav Zubok](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladislav_Zubok "Vladislav Zubok") stated: 'The collapse of the [Soviet empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_empire "Soviet empire") was an event of epochal geopolitical, military, ideological, and economic significance.'<sup id="cite_ref-479"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-479">[460]</a></sup>
