After the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union"), Russia drastically cut [military spending](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures "List of countries by military expenditures"), and restructuring the economy left millions unemployed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPBS2014_480-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPBS2014-480">[461]</a></sup> According to Western analysis, the neoliberal reforms in Russia culminated in a [recession](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recession "Recession") in the early 1990s more severe than the [Great Depression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Depression "Great Depression") as experienced by the United States and Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENolan199517–18_481-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTENolan199517%E2%80%9318-481">[462]</a></sup> Western analysts suggest that in the 25 years following the end of the Cold War, only five or six of the post-communist states are on a path to joining the rich and capitalist world while most are falling behind, some to such an extent that it will take several decades to catch up to where they were before the collapse of communism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhodsee201763_482-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhodsee201763-482">[463]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMilanović2015135–138_483-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMilanovi%C4%872015135%E2%80%93138-483">[464]</a></sup>

Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania consider themselves as [revivals of the three independent countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_continuity_of_the_Baltic_states "State continuity of the Baltic states") that existed prior to their [occupation and annexation by the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_occupation_of_the_Baltic_states_(1940) "Soviet occupation of the Baltic states (1940)") in 1940. They maintain that the process by which they were incorporated into the Soviet Union violated both international law and their own law, and that in 1990–1991 they were reasserting an independence that still legally existed.

Communist parties outside the Baltic states were not outlawed and their members were not prosecuted. Just a few places attempted to exclude members of communist secret services from decision-making. In some countries, the communist party changed its name and continued to function.<sup id="cite_ref-484"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-484">[465]</a></sup>

### Decommunization

[Stephen Holmes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Holmes_(political_scientist) "Stephen Holmes (political scientist)") of the [University of Chicago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Chicago "University of Chicago") argued in 1996 that decommunization, after a brief active period, quickly ended in near-universal failure. After the introduction of [lustration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lustration "Lustration"), demand for scapegoats has become relatively low, and former communists have been elected for high governmental and other administrative positions. Holmes notes that the only real exception was former [East Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Germany "East Germany"), where thousands of former [Stasi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stasi "Stasi") informers have been fired from public positions.<sup id="cite_ref-mandelbaum_485-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-mandelbaum-485">[466]</a></sup>

Holmes suggests the following reasons for the failure of decommunization:<sup id="cite_ref-mandelbaum_485-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-mandelbaum-485">[466]</a></sup>

-   After 45–70 years of communist rule, nearly every family has members associated with the state. After the initial desire "to root out the reds" came a realization that massive punishment is wrong and finding only some guilty is hardly justice.
-   The urgency of the current economic problems of postcommunism makes the crimes of the communist past "old news" for many citizens.
-   Decommunization is believed to be a power game of elites.
-   The difficulty of dislodging the social elite makes it require a [totalitarian state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Totalitarian_state "Totalitarian state") to disenfranchise the "[enemies of the people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enemies_of_the_people "Enemies of the people")" quickly and efficiently and a desire for normalcy overcomes the desire for punitive justice.
-   Very few people have a perfectly clean slate and so are available to fill the positions that require significant expertise.

Compared with the [decommunization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decommunization "Decommunization") efforts of the other former constituents of the [Eastern Bloc](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Bloc "Eastern Bloc") and the [Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union"), decommunization in Russia has been restricted to half-measures, if conducted at all.<sup id="cite_ref-486"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-486">[467]</a></sup> Notable anti-communist measures in the Russian Federation include the banning of the [Communist Party of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union "Communist Party of the Soviet Union") (and the creation of the [Communist Party of the Russian Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Russian_Federation "Communist Party of the Russian Federation")) as well as changing the names of some Russian cities back to what they were before the 1917 [October Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/October_Revolution "October Revolution") (Leningrad to [Saint Petersburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint_Petersburg "Saint Petersburg"), Sverdlovsk to [Yekaterinburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yekaterinburg "Yekaterinburg") and Gorky to [Nizhny Novgorod](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nizhny_Novgorod "Nizhny Novgorod")),<sup id="cite_ref-Goodbye,_Lenin:_Ukraine_moves_to_ban_communist_symbols_487-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Goodbye,_Lenin:_Ukraine_moves_to_ban_communist_symbols-487">[468]</a></sup> though others were maintained, with [Ulyanovsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulyanovsk "Ulyanovsk") (former Simbirsk), [Tolyatti](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tolyatti "Tolyatti") (former Stavropol) and [Kirov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kirov,_Kirov_Oblast "Kirov, Kirov Oblast") (former Vyatka) being examples. Even though Leningrad and Sverdlovsk were renamed, regions that were named after them are still officially called Leningrad and Sverdlovsk oblasts.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/The_Spasskaya_Tower%2C_photographed_in_1883%2C_published_1884_%28Saviour_Tower_-_%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B1%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D1%8F%29%2C_Moscow_Kremlin%2C_Red_Square.jpg/170px-The_Spasskaya_Tower%2C_photographed_in_1883%2C_published_1884_%28Saviour_Tower_-_%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B1%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D1%8F%29%2C_Moscow_Kremlin%2C_Red_Square.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_Spasskaya_Tower,_photographed_in_1883,_published_1884_(Saviour_Tower_-_%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B1%D0%B0%D1%88%D0%BD%D1%8F),_Moscow_Kremlin,_Red_Square.jpg)

The [Spasskaya Tower](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spasskaya_Tower "Spasskaya Tower") had kept its red star and did not restore the two-headed eagle present before communist takeover.

[Nostalgia for the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nostalgia_for_the_Soviet_Union "Nostalgia for the Soviet Union") is gradually on the rise in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Russians_with_fond_memories_of_the_USSR_488-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-The_Russians_with_fond_memories_of_the_USSR-488">[469]</a></sup> Communist symbols continue to form an important part of the rhetoric used in [state-controlled media](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Media_of_Russia "Media of Russia"), as banning on them in other countries is seen by the [Russian foreign ministry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Russia) "Ministry of Foreign Affairs (Russia)") as "sacrilege" and "a perverse idea of good and evil".<sup id="cite_ref-Goodbye,_Lenin:_Ukraine_moves_to_ban_communist_symbols_487-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Goodbye,_Lenin:_Ukraine_moves_to_ban_communist_symbols-487">[468]</a></sup> The process of [decommunization in Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decommunization_in_Ukraine "Decommunization in Ukraine"), a neighbouring [post-Soviet state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-Soviet_state "Post-Soviet state"), was met with fierce criticism by Russia,<sup id="cite_ref-Goodbye,_Lenin:_Ukraine_moves_to_ban_communist_symbols_487-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Goodbye,_Lenin:_Ukraine_moves_to_ban_communist_symbols-487">[468]</a></sup> who regularly dismisses [Soviet war crimes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soviet_war_crimes "Soviet war crimes").<sup id="cite_ref-The_rape_of_Berlin_489-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-The_rape_of_Berlin-489">[470]</a></sup> The [State Anthem of the Russian Federation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_Anthem_of_the_Russian_Federation "State Anthem of the Russian Federation"), adopted in 2000 (the same year [Vladimir Putin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Putin "Vladimir Putin") began his first term as president of Russia), uses the exact same music as the [State Anthem of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_Anthem_of_the_Soviet_Union "State Anthem of the Soviet Union"), but with new lyrics written by [Sergey Mikhalkov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sergey_Mikhalkov "Sergey Mikhalkov").

Conversely, decommunization in Ukraine started during and after the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union") in 1991<sup id="cite_ref-BBC8380433_490-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-BBC8380433-490">[471]</a></sup> With the success of the [Revolution of Dignity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolution_of_Dignity "Revolution of Dignity") in 2014, the [Ukrainian government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainian_government "Ukrainian government") approved [laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ukrainian_decommunization_laws "Ukrainian decommunization laws") that outlawed [communist symbols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_symbol "Communist symbol").<sup id="cite_ref-ectniiU_491-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-ectniiU-491">[472]</a></sup>

On 15 May 2015, President of Ukraine [Petro Poroshenko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petro_Poroshenko "Petro Poroshenko") signed a set of laws that started a six-month period for the removal of communist monuments (excluding [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II") monuments) and renaming of public places named after communist-related themes.<sup id="cite_ref-decommunizaion_492-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-decommunizaion-492">[473]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc32267075_493-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-bbc32267075-493">[474]</a></sup> At the time, this meant that 22 cities and 44 villages were set to get new names.<sup id="cite_ref-22_cities_and_44_villages_494-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-22_cities_and_44_villages-494">[475]</a></sup> Until 21 November 2015, municipal governments had the authority to implement this;<sup id="cite_ref-depo.ua_new_names_Ukraine_495-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-depo.ua_new_names_Ukraine-495">[476]</a></sup> if they failed to do so, the [Oblasts of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oblasts_of_Ukraine "Oblasts of Ukraine") had until 21 May 2016 to change the names.<sup id="cite_ref-depo.ua_new_names_Ukraine_495-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-depo.ua_new_names_Ukraine-495">[476]</a></sup> If after that date the settlement had retained its old name, the [Cabinet of Ministers of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cabinet_of_Ministers_of_Ukraine "Cabinet of Ministers of Ukraine") would wield authority to assign a new name to the settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-depo.ua_new_names_Ukraine_495-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-depo.ua_new_names_Ukraine-495">[476]</a></sup> In 2016, 51,493 streets and 987 cities and villages were renamed, and 1,320 [Lenin monuments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lenin_monuments "Lenin monuments") and 1,069 monuments to other communist figures removed.<sup id="cite_ref-rdiU16_496-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-rdiU16-496">[477]</a></sup> Violation of the law carries a penalty of a potential media ban and prison sentences of up to five years.<sup id="cite_ref-dwdc9415_497-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-dwdc9415-497">[478]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-oscedc18515_498-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-oscedc18515-498">[479]</a></sup>

On 24 July 2015, the [Ministry of the Interior](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ministry_of_the_Interior_(Ukraine) "Ministry of the Interior (Ukraine)") stripped the [Communist Party of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Ukraine "Communist Party of Ukraine"), the [Communist Party of Ukraine (renewed)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Ukraine_(renewed) "Communist Party of Ukraine (renewed)"), and the [Communist Party of Workers and Peasants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Workers_and_Peasants "Communist Party of Workers and Peasants") of their right to participate in elections and stated it was continuing the court actions that started in July 2014 to end the registration of [communist parties in Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Category:Communist_parties_in_Ukraine "Category:Communist parties in Ukraine").<sup id="cite_ref-Banukcom24715_499-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-Banukcom24715-499">[480]</a></sup> By 16 December 2015, these three parties had been banned in Ukraine; the Communist Party of Ukraine appealed the ban to the [European Court of Human Rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights "European Court of Human Rights").<sup id="cite_ref-500"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-500">[481]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-501"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-501">[482]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-502"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-502">[483]</a></sup>

## Influence

The Cold War continues to influence world affairs. The post-Cold War world is considered to be [unipolar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polarity_(international_relations)#Unipolarity "Polarity (international relations)"), with the United States the sole remaining [superpower](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superpower "Superpower").<sup id="cite_ref-503"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-503">[T]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlum200687_504-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlum200687-504">[484]</a></sup> The Cold War defined the political role of the United States after World War II—by 1989 the United States had military alliances with 50 countries, with 526,000 troops stationed abroad,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPBS2004_505-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPBS2004-505">[485]</a></sup> with 326,000 in Europe (two-thirds of which were in [West Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Germany "West Germany"))<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuke1989175_506-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuke1989175-506">[486]</a></sup> and 130,000 in Asia (mainly [Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japan "Japan") and [South Korea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Korea "South Korea")).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPBS2004_505-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPBS2004-505">[485]</a></sup> The Cold War also marked the zenith of peacetime [military–industrial complexes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military%E2%80%93industrial_complex "Military–industrial complex"), especially in the Soviet Union and the United States, and large-scale [military funding of science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_military_technology "History of military technology").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002_507-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002-507">[487]</a></sup> These complexes, though their origins may be found as early as the 19th century, snowballed considerably during the Cold War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPavelec2009xv–xvi_508-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPavelec2009xv%E2%80%93xvi-508">[488]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Enlargement_of_the_European_Union_77.gif/220px-Enlargement_of_the_European_Union_77.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Enlargement_of_the_European_Union_77.gif)

Since the end of the Cold War, the [EU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union "European Union") has [expanded eastwards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_European_Union "Enlargement of the European Union") into the former Warsaw Pact and parts of the former Soviet Union.

Cumulative US military expenditures throughout the entire Cold War amounted to an estimated $8 trillion. Further nearly 100,000 Americans died in the [Korean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korean_War "Korean War") and [Vietnam Wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnam_War "Vietnam War").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaFeber20021_509-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaFeber20021-509">[489]</a></sup> Although Soviet casualties are difficult to estimate, as a share of gross national product the financial cost for the Soviet Union was much higher than that incurred by the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005213_510-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005213-510">[490]</a></sup>

In addition to the loss of life by uniformed soldiers, millions died in the superpowers' [proxy wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proxy_war "Proxy war") around the globe, most notably in eastern Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005266_511-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaddis2005266-511">[491]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-512"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-512">[492]</a></sup> Most of the proxy wars and subsidies for local conflicts ended along with the Cold War; interstate wars, ethnic wars, revolutionary wars, as well as refugee and displaced persons crises have declined sharply in the post-Cold War years.<sup id="cite_ref-513"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-513">[U]</a></sup>

However, the aftermath of the Cold War is not considered to be concluded. Many of the economic and social tensions that were exploited to fuel Cold War competition in parts of the Third World remain acute. The breakdown of state control in a number of areas formerly ruled by communist governments produced new civil and ethnic conflicts, particularly in the former [Yugoslavia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yugoslavia "Yugoslavia"). In Central and Eastern Europe, the end of the Cold War has ushered in an era of [economic growth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_growth "Economic growth") and an increase in the number of [liberal democracies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberal_democracy "Liberal democracy"), while in other parts of the world, such as Afghanistan, independence was accompanied by [state failure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Failed_state "Failed state").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliday20012e_391-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliday20012e-391">[373]</a></sup>

## In popular culture

During the Cold War, the United States and the Soviet Union invested heavily in propaganda designed to influence people around the world, especially using motion pictures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShawYoungblood2010ch._1_514-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShawYoungblood2010ch._1-514">[493]</a></sup><sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2015)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Cold War endures as a popular topic reflected in entertainment media, and continuing to the present with post-1991 Cold War-themed feature films, novels, television and web series, and other media. In 2013, a KGB-sleeper-agents-living-next-door action drama series, _[The Americans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Americans "The Americans")_, set in the early 1980s, was ranked No. 6 on the [Metacritic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metacritic "Metacritic") annual Best New TV Shows list; its six-season run concluded in May 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-515"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-515">[494]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-516"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-516">[495]</a></sup>

## Historiography

As soon as the term "Cold War" was popularized to refer to post-war tensions between the United States and the Soviet Union, interpreting the course and origins of the conflict has been a source of heated controversy among historians, political scientists, and journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENashel1999_517-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTENashel1999-517">[496]</a></sup> In particular, historians have sharply disagreed as to who was responsible for the breakdown of Soviet–US relations after the Second World War; and whether the conflict between the two superpowers was inevitable or could have been avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmbroseBrinkley2011789–799_518-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmbroseBrinkley2011789%E2%80%93799-518">[497]</a></sup> Historians have also disagreed on what exactly the Cold War was, what the sources of the conflict were, and how to disentangle patterns of action and reaction between the two sides.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalliday20012e_391-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalliday20012e-391">[373]</a></sup>

Although explanations of the origins of the conflict in academic discussions are complex and diverse, several general schools of thought on the subject can be identified. Historians commonly speak of three different approaches to the study of the Cold War: "orthodox" accounts, "revisionism", and "post-revisionism".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002_507-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002-507">[487]</a></sup>

"Orthodox" accounts place responsibility for the Cold War on the Soviet Union and its expansion further into Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002_507-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002-507">[487]</a></sup> "Revisionist" writers place more responsibility for the breakdown of post-war peace on the United States, citing a range of US efforts to isolate and confront the Soviet Union well before the end of World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002_507-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002-507">[487]</a></sup> "Post-revisionists" see the events of the Cold War as more nuanced and attempt to be more balanced in determining what occurred during the Cold War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002_507-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun2002-507">[487]</a></sup> Much of the historiography on the Cold War weaves together two or even all three of these broad categories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEByrd2003_78-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_note-FOOTNOTEByrd2003-78">[72]</a></sup>

Frustrated and hoping to discredit him, the KGB initially planted false stories that Amin was a CIA agent. In the autumn these rumors rebounded on the KGB in a strange case of "[blowback](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blowback_(intelligence) "Blowback (intelligence)")," the term used by spies to describe planted propaganda that filters back to confuse the country that first set the story loose.

492.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War#cite_ref-512 "Jump up")** Kim, Samuel S. (2014). "The Evolving Asian System". _International Relations of Asia_. [Rowman & Littlefield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rowman_%26_Littlefield "Rowman & Littlefield"). p. 45. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9781442226418](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781442226418 "Special:BookSources/9781442226418"). With three of the four major Cold War fault lines—divided Germany, divided Korea, divided China, and divided Vietnam—East Asia acquired the dubious distinction of having engendered the largest number of armed conflicts resulting in higher fatalities between 1945 and 1994 than any other region or sub-region. Even in Asia, while Central and South Asia produced a regional total of 2.8 million in human fatalities, East Asia's regional total is 10.4 million including the Chinese Civil War (1 million), the Korean War (3 million), the Vietnam War (2 million), and the Pol Pot genocide in Cambodia (1 to 2 million).

[National Academies Press](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Academies_Press "National Academies Press"). pp. 102–105. [ISBN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-309-07334-9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-309-07334-9 "Special:BookSources/978-0-309-07334-9"). As best as can now be estimated, over two million Cambodians died during the 1970s because of the political events of the decade, the vast majority of them during the mere four years of the 'Khmer Rouge' regime. This number of deaths is even more staggering when related to the size of the Cambodian population, then less than eight million. ... Subsequent reevaluations of the demographic data situated the death toll for the \[civil war\] in the order of 300,000 or less.

Retrieved 6 June 2018. a US Embassy official in Jakarta, Robert Martens, had supplied the Indonesian Army with lists containing the names of thousands of PKI officials in the months after the alleged coup attempt. According to the journalist Kathy Kadane, "As many as 5,000 names were furnished over a period of months to the Army there, and the Americans later checked off the names of those who had been killed or captured." Despite Martens later denials of any such intent, these actions almost certainly aided in the death or detention of many innocent people. They also sent a powerful message that the US government agreed with and supported the army's campaign against the PKI, even as that campaign took its terrible toll in human lives

Washington did everything in its power to encourage and facilitate the army-led massacre of alleged PKI members, and U.S. officials worried only that the killing of the party's unarmed supporters might not go far enough, permitting Sukarno to return to power and frustrate the \[Johnson\] Administration's emerging plans for a post-Sukarno Indonesia. This was efficacious terror, an essential building block of the [neoliberal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoliberal "Neoliberal") policies that the West would attempt to impose on Indonesia after Sukarno's ouster.
